          <img alt="Katagalugan.png" data-file-height="3120" data-file-width="2000" height="343" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Katagalugan.png/220px-Katagalugan.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Katagalugan.png/330px-Katagalugan.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Katagalugan.png/440px-Katagalugan.png 2x" width="220"/>
         </a>
         <div style="text-align:left;">
          Predominantly Tagalog-speaking regions in the Philippines. The color-schemes represent the four dialect zones of the language: Northern, Central, Southern, and
          <a href="/wiki/Marinduque" title="Marinduque">
           Marinduque
          </a>
          The majority of residents in
          <a href="/wiki/Camarines_Norte" title="Camarines Norte">
           Camarines Norte
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/Camarines_Sur" title="Camarines Sur">
           Camarines Sur
          </a>
          speak
          <a href="/wiki/Bikol_languages" title="Bikol languages">
           Bikol
          </a>
          as their first language but these provinces nonetheless have significant Tagalog minorities. In addition, Tagalog is used as a
          <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">
           second language
          </a>
          across the Philippines.
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="noprint selfref" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#ddddff;padding:0.3em 0.5em;text-align:left;line-height:1.3;">
         <b>
          This article contains
          <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
           IPA
          </a>
          phonetic symbols.
         </b>
         Without proper
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Fonts" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          rendering support
         </a>
         , you may see
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Replacement_character" title="Replacement character">
          question marks, boxes, or other symbols
         </a>
         instead of
         <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
          Unicode
         </a>
         characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
          Help:IPA
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Tagalog
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">
            ɡ
           </span>
           <span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">
            ɑː
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">
            ɒ
           </span>
           <span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">
            ɡ
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <small>
        Tagalog pronunciation:
       </small>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Tagalog" title="Help:IPA/Tagalog">
         [tɐˈɡaːloɡ]
        </a>
       </span>
       ) is an
       <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">
        Austronesian language
       </a>
       spoken as a first language by a quarter of the population of the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
        Philippines
       </a>
       and as a second language by the majority. Its standardized form,
       <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">
        officially
       </a>
       named
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">
         Filipino
        </a>
       </i>
       , is the
       <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">
        national language
       </a>
       of the Philippines, and is one of two official languages alongside
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_English" title="Philippine English">
        English
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       It is related to other
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_languages" title="Philippine languages">
        Philippine languages
       </a>
       , such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Bikol_languages" title="Bikol languages">
        Bikol languages
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ilocano_language" title="Ilocano language">
        Ilocano
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Visayan_languages" title="Visayan languages">
        Visayan languages
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kapampangan_language" title="Kapampangan language">
        Kapampangan
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Pangasinan_language" title="Pangasinan language">
        Pangasinan
       </a>
       , and more distantly to other Austronesian languages, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Formosan_languages" title="Formosan languages">
        Formosan languages
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">
        Taiwan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">
        Malay
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_language" title="Malaysian language">
        Malaysian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">
        Indonesian
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_language" title="Hawaiian language">
        Hawaiian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="Māori language">
        Māori
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_language" title="Malagasy language">
        Malagasy
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Tagalog" title="Old Tagalog">
        Old Tagalog
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The word
       <i>
        Tagalog
       </i>
       is derived from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endonym" title="Endonym">
        endonym
       </a>
       <i>
        taga-log
       </i>
       ("river dweller"), composed of
       <i>
        tagá-
       </i>
       ("native of" or "from") and
       <i>
        ilog
       </i>
       ("river"). Linguists such as Dr. David Zorc and Dr.
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Blust" title="Robert Blust">
        Robert Blust
       </a>
       speculate that the Tagalogs and other Central Philippine ethno-linguistic groups originated in Northeastern
       <a href="/wiki/Mindanao" title="Mindanao">
        Mindanao
       </a>
       or the Eastern
       <a href="/wiki/Visayas" title="Visayas">
        Visayas
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first written record of Tagalog is the
       <a href="/wiki/Laguna_Copperplate_Inscription" title="Laguna Copperplate Inscription">
        Laguna Copperplate Inscription
       </a>
       , which dates to 900 CE and exhibits fragments of the language along with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">
        Sanskrit
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Malay" title="Old Malay">
        Old Malay
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Javanese_language" title="Javanese language">
        Javanese
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Tagalog" title="Old Tagalog">
        Old Tagalog
       </a>
       . The first known complete book to be written in Tagalog is the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Doctrina_Christiana" title="Doctrina Christiana">
         Doctrina Christiana
        </a>
       </i>
       (Christian Doctrine), printed in 1593. The
       <i>
        Doctrina
       </i>
       was written in Spanish and two transcriptions of Tagalog; one in the ancient, then-current
       <a href="/wiki/Baybayin" title="Baybayin">
        Baybayin
       </a>
       script and the other in an early Spanish attempt at a
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">
        Latin
       </a>
       orthography for the language.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baybayin_alpha.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="183" data-file-width="361" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Baybayin_alpha.jpg/300px-Baybayin_alpha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Baybayin_alpha.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baybayin_alpha.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Tagalog
         <a href="/wiki/Baybayin" title="Baybayin">
          Baybayin
         </a>
         script.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Throughout the 333 years of Spanish rule, various grammars and dictionaries were written by Spanish clergymen, including
       <i>
        Vocabulario de la Lengua Tagala
       </i>
       by Pedro de San Buenaventura (
       <a href="/wiki/Pila,_Laguna" title="Pila, Laguna">
        Pila, Laguna
       </a>
       , 1613), the Czech
       <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Clain" title="Pablo Clain">
        Paul Klein
       </a>
       <i>
        Vocabulario de la lengua tagala
       </i>
       (beginning of the 18th century),
       <i>
        Vocabulario de la lengua tagala
       </i>
       (1835), and
       <i>
        Arte de la lengua tagala y manual tagalog para la administración de los Santos Sacramentos
       </i>
       (1850) in addition to early studies
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of the language; however, the indigenous poet
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francisco_Baltazar" title="Francisco Baltazar">
        Francisco Baltazar
       </a>
       (1788–1862) is regarded as the foremost Tagalog writer, his most notable work being the early 19th-century
       <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">
        epic
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Florante_at_Laura" title="Florante at Laura">
         Florante at Laura
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-google-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_changes">
        Historical changes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Historical changes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diariong_Tagalog.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="331" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Diariong_Tagalog.jpg/220px-Diariong_Tagalog.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Diariong_Tagalog.jpg/330px-Diariong_Tagalog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/Diariong_Tagalog.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diariong_Tagalog.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://tl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diariong_Tagalog" title="tl:Diariong Tagalog">
           Diariong Tagalog
          </a>
         </i>
         (Tagalog Newspaper), the first bilingual newspaper in the Philippines founded in 1882 written in both Tagalog and
         <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
          Spanish
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Tagalog differs from its
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Philippine_languages" title="Central Philippine languages">
        Central Philippine
       </a>
       counterparts with its treatment of the
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Philippine_language" title="Proto-Philippine language">
        Proto-Philippine
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Schwa" title="Schwa">
        schwa
       </a>
       vowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        *ə
       </span>
       . In most
       <a href="/wiki/Bikol_languages" title="Bikol languages">
        Bikol
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Visayan_languages" title="Visayan languages">
        Visayan
       </a>
       languages, this sound merged with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /u/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [o]
       </span>
       . In Tagalog, it has merged with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       . For example, Proto-Philippine
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        *dəkət
       </span>
       (adhere, stick) is Tagalog
       <i>
        dikít
       </i>
       and Visayan &amp; Bikol
       <i>
        dukot
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Proto-Philippine
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        *r
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        *j
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        *z
       </span>
       merged with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /d/
       </span>
       but is
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /l/
       </span>
       between vowels. Proto-Philippine
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        *ŋajan
       </span>
       (name) and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        *hajək
       </span>
       (kiss) became Tagalog
       <i>
        ngalan
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        halík
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Proto-Philippine
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        *R
       </span>
       merged with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɡ/
       </span>
       .
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        *tubiR
       </span>
       (water) and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        *zuRuʔ
       </span>
       (blood) became Tagalog
       <i>
        tubig
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        dugô
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">
        Official status
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Official status">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">
        Filipino language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The first substantial dictionary of the Tagalog language was written by the
       <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">
        Czech
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">
        Jesuit
       </a>
       missionary
       <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Clain" title="Pablo Clain">
        Pablo Clain
       </a>
       in the beginning of the 18th century. Clain spoke Tagalog and used it actively in several of his books. He wrote the first dictionary, which he later passed over to Francisco Jansens and José Hernandez.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Further compilation of his substantial work was prepared by P. Juan de Noceda and P. Pedro de Sanlucar and published as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Vocabulario_de_la_Lengua_Tagala" title="Vocabulario de la Lengua Tagala">
         Vocabulario de la Lengua Tagala
        </a>
       </i>
       in Manila in 1754 and then repeatedly
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       reedited, with the last edition being in 2013 in Manila.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Tagalog was declared the official language by the first constitution in the Philippines, the
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Provisional_Constitution_of_the_Philippines_(1897)#ARTICLE_VIII:_OFFICIAL_LANGUAGE" title="wikisource:Provisional Constitution of the Philippines (1897)">
        Constitution of Biak-na-Bato
       </a>
       in 1897.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1935, the Philippine constitution designated English and Spanish as official languages, but mandated the development and adoption of a common national language based on one of the existing native languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After study and deliberation, the National Language Institute, a committee composed of seven members who represented various regions in the Philippines, chose Tagalog as the basis for the evolution and adoption of the national language of the Philippines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLQspeech_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MLQspeech-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalez_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       President Manuel L. Quezon then, on December 30, 1937, proclaimed the selection of the Tagalog language to be used as the basis for the evolution and adoption of the national language of the Philippines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLQspeech_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-MLQspeech-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1939, President Quezon renamed the proposed Tagalog-based national language as
       <i>
        wikang pambansâ
       </i>
       (national language).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalez_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1959, the language was further renamed as "Pilipino".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalez_17-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 1973 constitution designated the Tagalog-based "Pilipino", along with English, as an official language and mandated the development and formal adoption of a common national language to be known as Filipino.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 1987 constitution designated Filipino as the national language mandating that as it evolves, it shall be further developed and enriched on the basis of existing Philippine and other languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1987constitutionXIV_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-1987constitutionXIV-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Article XIV, Section 7 of the 1987
       <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Philippines" title="Constitution of the Philippines">
        Constitution of the Philippines
       </a>
       specifies, in part:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Subject to provisions of law and as the Congress may deem appropriate, the Government shall take steps to initiate and sustain the use of Filipino as a medium of official communication and as language of instruction in the educational system.
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1987constitutionXIV_19-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-1987constitutionXIV-19">
           [19]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        The regional languages are the auxiliary official languages in the regions and shall serve as auxiliary media of instruction therein.
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1987constitutionXIV_19-2">
          <a href="#cite_note-1987constitutionXIV-19">
           [19]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       In 2009, the
       <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Education_(Philippines)" title="Department of Education (Philippines)">
        Department of Education
       </a>
       promulgated an order institutionalizing a system of mother-tongue based multilingual education ("MLE"), wherein instruction is conducted primarily in a student's mother tongue until at least grade three, with additional languages such as Filipino and English being introduced as separate subjects no earlier than grade two. In secondary school, Filipino and English become the primary languages of instruction, with the learner's first language taking on an auxiliary role.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It is the first language of a quarter of the population of the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
        Philippines
       </a>
       and a second language of the majority.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philippine_Census_2000_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Philippine_Census_2000-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Tagalog is a
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Philippine_languages" title="Central Philippine languages">
        Central Philippine
       </a>
       language within the
       <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">
        Austronesian
       </a>
       language family. Being
       <a href="/wiki/Malayo-Polynesian_languages" title="Malayo-Polynesian languages">
        Malayo-Polynesian
       </a>
       , it is related to other Austronesian languages, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_language" title="Malagasy language">
        Malagasy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Javanese_language" title="Javanese language">
        Javanese
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">
        Malay
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_language" title="Malaysian language">
        Malaysian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">
        Indonesian
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Tetum_language" title="Tetum language">
        Tetum
       </a>
       (of Timor), and
       <a href="/wiki/Yami_language" title="Yami language">
        Yami
       </a>
       (of Taiwan).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnologue.com_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ethnologue.com-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is closely related to the languages spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/Bicol_Region" title="Bicol Region">
        Bicol Region
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Visayas" title="Visayas">
        Visayas
       </a>
       islands, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Bikol_languages" title="Bikol languages">
        Bikol group
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Visayan_languages" title="Visayan languages">
        Visayan group
       </a>
       , including
       <a href="/wiki/Hiligaynon_language" title="Hiligaynon language">
        Hiligaynon
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cebuano_language" title="Cebuano language">
        Cebuano
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnologue.com_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ethnologue.com-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">
        Dialects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dialects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CalauanChurchjf4373_11.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/CalauanChurchjf4373_11.JPG/250px-CalauanChurchjf4373_11.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/CalauanChurchjf4373_11.JPG/375px-CalauanChurchjf4373_11.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/CalauanChurchjf4373_11.JPG/500px-CalauanChurchjf4373_11.JPG 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CalauanChurchjf4373_11.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">
          Ten Commandments
         </a>
         in Tagalog.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       At present, no comprehensive
       <a href="/wiki/Dialectology" title="Dialectology">
        dialectology
       </a>
       has been done in the Tagalog-speaking regions, though there have been descriptions in the form of dictionaries and grammars of various Tagalog dialects.
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">
        Ethnologue
       </a>
       lists Lubang, Manila,
       <a href="/wiki/Marinduque" title="Marinduque">
        Marinduque
       </a>
       , Bataan,
       <a href="/wiki/Batangas_Tagalog" title="Batangas Tagalog">
        Batangas
       </a>
       , Bulacan, Tanay-Paete (Rizal-Laguna), and Tayabas as dialects of Tagalog; however, there appear to be four main dialects, of which the aforementioned are a part: Northern (exemplified by the
       <a href="/wiki/Bulacan" title="Bulacan">
        Bulacan
       </a>
       dialect), Central (including Manila), Southern (exemplified by Batangas), and Marinduque.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some example of dialectal differences are:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Many Tagalog dialects, particularly those in the south, preserve the glottal stop found after consonants and before vowels. This has been lost in Standard Tagalog. For example, standard Tagalog
        <i>
         ngayón
        </i>
        (now, today),
        <i>
         sinigáng
        </i>
        (broth stew),
        <i>
         gabí
        </i>
        (night),
        <i>
         matamís
        </i>
        (sweet), are pronounced and written
        <i>
         ngay-on
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         sinig-ang
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         gab-i
        </i>
        , and
        <i>
         matam-is
        </i>
        in other dialects.
       </li>
       <li>
        In
        <a href="/wiki/Teresa,_Rizal" title="Teresa, Rizal">
         Teresian
        </a>
        -
        <a href="/wiki/Morong,_Rizal" title="Morong, Rizal">
         Morong
        </a>
        Tagalog,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɾ]
        </span>
        is usually preferred over
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [d]
        </span>
        . For example,
        <i>
         bundók
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         dagat
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         dingdíng
        </i>
        , and
        <i>
         isdâ
        </i>
        become
        <i>
         bunrók
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         ragat
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         ringríng
        </i>
        , and
        <i>
         isrâ
        </i>
        , e.g. "sandók sa dingdíng" becoming "sanrók sa ringríng".
       </li>
       <li>
        In many
        <a href="/wiki/Batangas_Tagalog" title="Batangas Tagalog">
         southern dialects
        </a>
        , the progressive aspect infix of
        <i>
         -um-
        </i>
        verbs is
        <i>
         na-
        </i>
        . For example, standard Tagalog
        <i>
         kumakain
        </i>
        (eating) is
        <i>
         nákáin
        </i>
        in Quezon and Batangas Tagalog. This is the butt of some jokes by other Tagalog speakers, for should a Southern Tagalog ask
        <i>
         nákáin ka ba ng patíng?
        </i>
        ("Do you eat shark?"), he would be understood as saying "Has a shark eaten you?" by speakers of the Manila Dialect.
       </li>
       <li>
        Some dialects have interjections which are considered a regional trademark. For example, the interjection
        <i>
         ala e!
        </i>
        usually identifies someone from Batangas as does
        <i>
         hane?!
        </i>
        in Rizal and Quezon provinces.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Perhaps the most divergent Tagalog dialects are those spoken in Marinduque.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Linguist Rosa Soberano identifies two dialects, western and eastern, with the former being closer to the Tagalog dialects spoken in the provinces of Batangas and Quezon.
      </p>
      <p>
       One example is the verb conjugation paradigms. While some of the affixes are different, Marinduque also preserves the imperative affixes, also found in Visayan and Bikol languages, that have mostly disappeared from most Tagalog early 20th century; they have since merged with the infinitive.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th style="background:#efefef;">
         <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">
          Manila Tagalog
         </a>
        </th>
        <th style="background:#efefef;">
         Marinduqueño Tagalog
        </th>
        <th style="background:#efefef;">
         English
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Susulat siná María at Esperanza kay Juan.
        </td>
        <td>
         Másúlat da María at Esperanza kay Juan.
        </td>
        <td>
         "María and Esperanza will write to Juan."
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Mag-aaral siya sa Maynilà.
        </td>
        <td>
         Gaaral siya sa Maynilà.
        </td>
        <td>
         "[He/She] will study in Manila."
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Maglutò ka na.
        </td>
        <td>
         Paglutò.
        </td>
        <td>
         "Cook now."
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Kainin mo iyán.
        </td>
        <td>
         Kaina yaan.
        </td>
        <td>
         "Eat it."
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Tinatawag tayo ni Tatay.
        </td>
        <td>
         Inatawag nganì kitá ni Tatay.
        </td>
        <td>
         "Father is calling us."
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Tútulungan ba kayó ni Hilario?
        </td>
        <td>
         Atulungan ga kamo ni Hilario?
        </td>
        <td>
         "Is Hilario going to help you?"
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Northern and central dialects form the basis for the national language.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">
        Geographic distribution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brgy._Santo_Angel_No_Dumping_Sign.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Brgy._Santo_Angel_No_Dumping_Sign.jpg/220px-Brgy._Santo_Angel_No_Dumping_Sign.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Brgy._Santo_Angel_No_Dumping_Sign.jpg/330px-Brgy._Santo_Angel_No_Dumping_Sign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Brgy._Santo_Angel_No_Dumping_Sign.jpg/440px-Brgy._Santo_Angel_No_Dumping_Sign.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brgy._Santo_Angel_No_Dumping_Sign.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         No dumping sign along the highway in the
         <a href="/wiki/Laguna_(province)" title="Laguna (province)">
          Laguna province
         </a>
         , Philippines.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BayLos_Ba%C3%B1osjf3918_09.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4608" data-file-width="3456" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/BayLos_Ba%C3%B1osjf3918_09.JPG/220px-BayLos_Ba%C3%B1osjf3918_09.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/BayLos_Ba%C3%B1osjf3918_09.JPG/330px-BayLos_Ba%C3%B1osjf3918_09.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/BayLos_Ba%C3%B1osjf3918_09.JPG/440px-BayLos_Ba%C3%B1osjf3918_09.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BayLos_Ba%C3%B1osjf3918_09.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Welcome sign in
         <a href="/wiki/Los_Ba%C3%B1os,_Laguna" title="Los Baños, Laguna">
          Los Baños, Laguna
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       According to the Philippine Statistics Authority, as of 2014 there were 100 million people living in the Philippines, where almost all of whom will have some basic level of understanding of the language. The Tagalog homeland,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Katagalugan" title="Katagalugan">
        Katagalugan
       </a>
       , covers roughly much of the central to southern parts of the island of
       <a href="/wiki/Luzon" title="Luzon">
        Luzon
       </a>
       —particularly in
       <a href="/wiki/Aurora_(province)" title="Aurora (province)">
        Aurora
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bataan" title="Bataan">
        Bataan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Batangas" title="Batangas">
        Batangas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bulacan" title="Bulacan">
        Bulacan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cavite" title="Cavite">
        Cavite
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Laguna_(province)" title="Laguna (province)">
        Laguna
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Metro_Manila" title="Metro Manila">
        Metro Manila
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nueva_Ecija" title="Nueva Ecija">
        Nueva Ecija
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Quezon" title="Quezon">
        Quezon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rizal" title="Rizal">
        Rizal
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Zambales" title="Zambales">
        Zambales
       </a>
       . Tagalog is also spoken natively by inhabitants living on the islands,
       <a href="/wiki/Marinduque" title="Marinduque">
        Marinduque
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mindoro" title="Mindoro">
        Mindoro
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Palawan" title="Palawan">
        Palawan
       </a>
       . It is spoken by approximately 64 million Filipinos, 96% of the household population;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       22 million, or 28% of the total Philippine population,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       speak it as a native language.
      </p>
      <p>
       Tagalog speakers are found in other parts of the Philippines as well as throughout the world, though its use is usually limited to communication between
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_the_Philippines" title="Ethnic groups in the Philippines">
        Filipino ethnic groups
       </a>
       . In
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       2010, the US Census bureau reported (based on data collected in 2007) that in the United States it was the fourth most-spoken language at home with almost 1.5 million speakers, behind
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish-based_creole_languages" title="Spanish-based creole languages">
        Spanish Creole
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       (including Patois, Cajun, Creole), and
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       . Tagalog ranked as the third most spoken language in metropolitan statistical areas, behind Spanish and Chinese but ahead of French.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-USA-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Accents">
        Accents
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Accents">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Tagalog language also boasts
       <a href="/wiki/Accent_(sociolinguistics)" title="Accent (sociolinguistics)">
        accentations
       </a>
       unique to some parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_people" title="Tagalog people">
        Tagalog-speaking regions
       </a>
       . For example, in some parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">
        Manila
       </a>
       , a strong pronunciation of
       <i>
        i
       </i>
       exists and vowel-switching of
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       exists so words like "gising" (to wake) is pronounced as "giseng" with a strong 'e' and the word "tagu-taguan" (hide-and-go-seek) is pronounced as "tago-tagoan" with a mild 'o'.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Batangas_Tagalog" title="Batangas Tagalog">
        Batangas Tagalog
       </a>
       boasts the most distinctive accent in Tagalog compared to the more Hispanized northern accents of the language.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Batangas" title="Batangas">
        Batangas
       </a>
       accent has been featured in film and television and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filipino_people" title="Filipino people">
        Filipino
       </a>
       actor
       <a href="/wiki/Leo_Martinez" title="Leo Martinez">
        Leo Martinez
       </a>
       speaks with this accent. Martinez's accent, however, will quickly be recognized by native Batangueños as representative of the accent in western Batangas which is milder compared to that used in the eastern part of the province.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Code-switching">
        Code-switching
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Code-switching">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Taglish" title="Taglish">
         Taglish
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Englog" title="Englog">
         Englog
        </a>
       </i>
       are names given to a mix of English and Tagalog. The amount of English vs. Tagalog varies from the occasional use of English loan words to outright
       <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">
        code-switching
       </a>
       , where the language changes in mid-sentence. Such code-switching is prevalent throughout the Philippines and in various languages of the Philippines other than Tagalog.
      </p>
      <p>
       Code-mixing also entails the use of foreign words that are "Filipinized" by reforming them using Filipino rules, such as verb conjugations. Users typically use Filipino or English words, whichever comes to mind first or whichever is easier to use.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        "Magshoshopping kami sa mall. Sino ba ang magdadrive sa shopping center?"
       </dd>
       <dd>
        "We will go shopping at the mall. Who will drive to the shopping center?"
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       City-dwellers, the highly educated, and people born around and after World War II are more likely to do this.
      </p>
      <p>
       The practice is common in television, radio, and print media as well. Advertisements from companies like
       <a href="/wiki/Wells_Fargo" title="Wells Fargo">
        Wells Fargo
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wal-Mart" title="Wal-Mart">
        Wal-Mart
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Albertsons" title="New Albertsons">
        Albertsons
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">
        McDonald's
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Union" title="Western Union">
        Western Union
       </a>
       have contained Taglish.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_phonology" title="Tagalog phonology">
        Tagalog phonology
       </a>
      </div>
      <table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          <img alt="[]" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="75" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/30px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/45px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/60px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 2x" width="30"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;">
         <b>
          This article contains
          <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
           IPA
          </a>
          phonetic symbols.
         </b>
         Without proper
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Fonts" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          rendering support
         </a>
         , you may see
         <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">
          question marks, boxes, or other symbols
         </a>
         instead of
         <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
          Unicode
         </a>
         characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
          Help:IPA
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Tagalog has 33
       <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
        phonemes
       </a>
       : 19 of them are
       <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">
        consonants
       </a>
       and 14 are
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">
        vowels
       </a>
       . Syllable structure is relatively simple, being maximally
       <i>
        consonant-ar-vowel-consonant
       </i>
       , where
       <i>
        consonant-ar
       </i>
       only occurs in borrowed words such as
       <i>
        trak
       </i>
       "truck" or
       <i>
        sombréro
       </i>
       "hat".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ELL_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ELL-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">
        Vowels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Vowels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Tagalog has ten simple vowels, five long and five short, and four diphthongs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ELL_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ELL-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Before appearing in the area north of Pasig river, Tagalog had three vowel qualities:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /u/
       </span>
       . This was later expanded to five with the introduction of words from Northern Philippine languages like
       <a href="/wiki/Kapampangan_language" title="Kapampangan language">
        Kapampangan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ilocano_language" title="Ilocano language">
        Ilocano
       </a>
       and Spanish words.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        <b>
         Table of the five general Tagalog vowel phonemes
        </b>
       </caption>
       <tr style="text-align:center;">
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
         Front
        </th>
        <th>
         Central
        </th>
        <th>
         Back
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align:center;">
        <th>
         Close
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">
           i
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨i⟩
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">
           u
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨u⟩
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align:center;">
        <th>
         Mid
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">
           ɛ
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨e⟩
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Mid back rounded vowel">
           o̞
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨o⟩
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align:center;">
        <th>
         Open
        </th>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">
           a
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨a⟩
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /a/
        </span>
        an
        <a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">
         open central unrounded vowel
        </a>
        roughly similar to English "f
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        ther"; in the middle of a word, a
        <a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_vowel" title="Near-open central vowel">
         near-open central vowel
        </a>
        similar to Received Pronunciation "c
        <b>
         u
        </b>
        p"; or an
        <a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">
         open front unrounded vowel
        </a>
        similar to Received Pronunciation or California English "h
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        t"
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɛ/
        </span>
        an
        <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">
         open-mid front unrounded vowel
        </a>
        similar to General American English "b
        <b>
         e
        </b>
        d"
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i/
        </span>
        a
        <a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">
         close front unrounded vowel
        </a>
        similar to English "mach
        <b>
         i
        </b>
        ne"
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /o/
        </span>
        a
        <a href="/wiki/Mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Mid back rounded vowel">
         mid back rounded vowel
        </a>
        similar to General American English "s
        <b>
         o
        </b>
        ul" or Philippine English "f
        <b>
         o
        </b>
        rty"
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /u/
        </span>
        a
        <a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">
         close back rounded vowel
        </a>
        similar to English "fl
        <b>
         u
        </b>
        te"
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Nevertheless, simplification of pairs
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [o ~ u]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɛ ~ i]
       </span>
       is likely to take place, especially in some Tagalog as second language, remote location and worker class registers.
      </p>
      <p>
       The four
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthongs
       </a>
       are
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /aj/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /uj/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /aw/
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /iw/
       </span>
       . Long vowels are not written apart from pedagogical texts, where an acute accent is used:
       <i>
        á é í ó ú.
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ELL_29-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ELL-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        <b>
         Table of all possible realizations of Tagalog vowels
        </b>
       </caption>
       <tr style="text-align:center;">

        <th>

           </a>
          </span>
         </b>
         ⟨i⟩
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>

          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨i⟩
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>

          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨u⟩
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align:center;">
        <th>
         Mid
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Mid front unrounded vowel">
           ɛ̝
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨e⟩
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           <a href="/wiki/Mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Mid back rounded vowel">
            o̞
           </a>
          </span>
         </b>
         ⟨o⟩
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align:center;">
        <th>
         Open-mid
        </th>
        <td>
         <b>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">
            ɛ
           </a>
          </span>
         </b>
         ⟨e⟩
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">
           ɔ
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨o⟩
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align:center;">
        <th>
         Near-open
        </th>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-open central unrounded vowel">
           ɐ
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨a⟩
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align:center;">
        <th>
         Open
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">
           a
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨a⟩
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           <a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">
            ä
           </a>
          </span>
         </b>
         ⟨a⟩
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The table above shows all the possible realizations for each of the five vowel sounds depending on the speaker's origin or proficiency. The five general vowels are in
       <b>
        bold
       </b>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>

          </b>
         </i>

        </td>
        <td style="border-left: 0;">
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <small>
          <a href="/wiki/Approximant_consonant" title="Approximant consonant">
           Approximant
          </a>
         </small>
        </th>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td style="border-right: 0;">
        </td>
        <td style="border-left: 0;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
           l
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨l⟩
        </td>
        <td style="border-right: 0;">
        </td>
        <td style="border-left: 0;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant#Palatal" title="Palatal approximant">
           j
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨y⟩
        </td>
        <td style="border-right: 0;">
        </td>
        <td style="border-left: 0;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Voiced_labio-velar_approximant" title="Voiced labio-velar approximant">
           w
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨w⟩
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <small>
          <a href="/wiki/Rhotic_consonant" title="Rhotic consonant">
           Rhotic
          </a>
         </small>
        </th>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td style="border-right: 0;">
        </td>
        <td style="border-left: 0;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_flap" title="Alveolar flap">
           ɾ
          </a>
         </span>
         ⟨r⟩
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /k/
        </span>
        between vowels has a tendency to become
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [x]
        </span>
        as in Spanish "José", whereas in the initial position it has a tendency to become
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [kx]
        </span>
        , especially in the Manila dialect.
       </li>
       <li>
        Intervocalic
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɡ/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /k/
        </span>
        tend to become
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɰ]
        </span>
        , as in Spanish "agua", especially in the Manila dialect.
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɾ/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /d/
        </span>
        were once allophones, and they still vary grammatically, with initial
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /d/
        </span>
        becoming intervocalic
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɾ/
        </span>
        in many words.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ELL_29-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-ELL-29">
          [29]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        A glottal stop that occurs in
        <a href="/wiki/Pausa" title="Pausa">
         pausa
        </a>
        (before a pause) is omitted when it is in the middle of a phrase,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ELL_29-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-ELL-29">
          [29]
         </a>
        </sup>
        especially in the Metro Manila area. The vowel it follows is then lengthened. However, it is preserved in many other dialects.
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɾ/
        </span>
        phoneme is an alveolar rhotic that has a free variation between a trill, a flap and an approximant (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [r~ɾ~ɹ]
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /dʒ/
        </span>
        phoneme may become a consonant cluster
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [dd͡ʒ]
        </span>
        in between vowels such as
        <i>
         sadyâ
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [sadˈd͡ʒäʔ]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Glottal stop is not indicated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ELL_29-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-ELL-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Glottal stops are most likely to occur when:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        the word starts with a vowel, like "
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        so" (dog)
       </li>
       <li>
        the word includes a dash followed by a vowel, like "mag-
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        ral" (study)
       </li>
       <li>
        the word has two vowels next to each other, like "pa
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        no" (how)
       </li>
       <li>
        the word starts with a prefix followed by a verb that starts with a vowel, like "mag-aayos" ([will] fix)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lexical_stress">
        Lexical stress
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Lexical stress">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lexical_stress" title="Lexical stress">
        Lexical stress
       </a>
       , coupled with
       <a href="/wiki/Glottalization" title="Glottalization">
        glottalization
       </a>
       , is a
       <a href="/wiki/Distinctive_feature" title="Distinctive feature">
        distinctive feature
       </a>
       in Tagalog. Primary stress normally occurs on either the final or the penultimate syllable of a word. Long vowel accompany primary or secondary stress unless the stress occurs at the end of a word.
      </p>
      <p>
       Tagalog words are often distinguished from one another by the position of the stress and the presence of the glottal stop. In general, there are four types of phonetic emphases, which, in formal or academic settings, are indicated with a
       <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">
        diacritic
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        tuldík
       </i>
       ) above the vowel. The penultimate primary stress position (
       <i>
        malumay
       </i>
       ) is the default stress type and so is left unwritten except in dictionaries. The name of each stress type has its corresponding diacritic in the final vowel.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="margin:autmargin:auto;">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_alignment" title="Austronesian alignment">
        Austronesian alignment
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_grammar" title="Tagalog grammar">
        Tagalog grammar
