      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">
        Writing system
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Writing system">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_orthography" title="Filipino orthography">
        Filipino orthography
       </a>
      </div>
      <table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="32" data-file-width="36" height="36" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Baybayin_Ba.svg/40px-Baybayin_Ba.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Baybayin_Ba.svg/60px-Baybayin_Ba.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Baybayin_Ba.svg/80px-Baybayin_Ba.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;">
         <b>
          This article contains
          <a href="/wiki/Baybayin" title="Baybayin">
           Baybayin
          </a>
          script.
         </b>
         Without proper
         <a href="/wiki/Help:Multilingual_support#Old_Tagalog.2FBaybayin" title="Help:Multilingual support">
          rendering support
         </a>
         , you may see
         <a href="/wiki/Mojibake" title="Mojibake">
          question marks, boxes, or other symbols
         </a>
         instead of Baybayin characters.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Tagalog, like other Philippines languages today, is written using the Latin alphabet. Prior to the arrival of the Spanish in 1521 and the beginning of their colonization in 1565, Tagalog was written in an
       <a href="/wiki/Abugida" title="Abugida">
        abugida
       </a>
       –or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alphasyllabary" title="Alphasyllabary">
        alphasyllabary
       </a>
       —called
       <a href="/wiki/Baybayin" title="Baybayin">
        Baybayin
       </a>
       . This system of writing gradually gave way to the use and propagation of the Latin alphabet as introduced by the Spanish. As the Spanish began to record and create grammars and dictionaries for the various languages of the Philippine archipelago, they adopted systems of writing closely following the orthographic customs of the Spanish langauge and were refined over the years. Until the first half of the 20th century, most Philippine languages were widely written in a variety of ways based on Spanish orthography.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the late 19th century, a number of educated Filipinos began proposing for revising the spelling system used for Tagalog at the time. In 1884, Filipino doctor and student of langauges
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_Pardo_de_Tavera" title="Trinidad Pardo de Tavera">
        Trinidad Pardo de Tavera
       </a>
       published his study on the ancient Tagalog script
       <i>
        Contribucion para el Estudio de los Antiguos Alfabetos Filipinos
       </i>
       and in 1887, published his essay
       <i>
        El Sanscrito en la lengua Tagalog
       </i>
       which made use of a new writing system developed by him. Meanwhile, Jose Rizal, inspired by Pardo de Tavera's 1884 work, also began developing a new system of orthography (unaware at first of Pardo de Tavera's own orthography).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Is_k_foreign_19_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Is_k_foreign_19-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A major noticeable change in these proposed orthographies was the use of the letter ⟨k⟩ rather than ⟨c⟩ and ⟨q⟩ to represent the phoneme /k/.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1889, the new bilingual Spanish-Tagalog
       <i>
        La España Oriental
       </i>
       newspaper, of which
       <a href="/wiki/Isabelo_de_los_Reyes" title="Isabelo de los Reyes">
        Isabelo de los Reyes
       </a>
       was an editor, began publishing using the new orthography stating in a footnote that it would "use the orthography recently introduced by ... learned Orientalis". This new orthography, while having its supporters, was also not initially accepted by several writers. Soon after the first issue of
       <i>
        La España
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pascual_H._Poblete" title="Pascual H. Poblete">
        Pascual H. Poblete
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        Revista Católica de Filipina
       </i>
       began a series of articles attacking the new orthography and its proponents. A fellow writer, Pablo Tecson was also critical. Among the attacks was the use of the letters "k" and "w" as they were deemed to be of German origin and thus its proponents were deemed as "unpatriotic". The publishers of these two papers would eventually merge as
       <i>
        La Lectura Popular
       </i>
       in January 1890 and would eventually make use of both spelling systems in its articles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k_is_for_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-k_is_for-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Is_k_foreign_19_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Is_k_foreign_19-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Pedro Laktaw, a schoolteacher, published the first Spanish-Tagalog dictionary using the new orthography in 1890.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k_is_for_31-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-k_is_for-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In April 1890, Jose Rizal authored an article
       <i>
        Sobre la Nueva Ortografia de la Lengua Tagalog
       </i>
       in the Madrid-based periodical
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Solidaridad" title="La Solidaridad">
        La Solidaridad
       </a>
       . In it, he addressed the criticisms of the new writing system by writers like Pobrete and Tecson and the simplicity, in his opinion, of the new orthography. Rizal described the orthography promoted by Pardo de Tavera as "more perfect" than what he himself had developed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k_is_for_31-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-k_is_for-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The new orthography was however not broadly adopted initially and was used inconsistently in the bilingual periodicals of Manila until the early 20th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k_is_for_31-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-k_is_for-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The revolutionary society Kataás-taasan, Kagalang-galang Katipunan ng̃ mg̃á Anak ng̃ Bayan or
       <a href="/wiki/Katipunan" title="Katipunan">
        Katipunan
       </a>
       made use of the k-orthography and the letter k featured prominently on many of its flags and insignias.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k_is_for_31-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-k_is_for-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1937, Tagalog was selected to serve as basis for the country's
       <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">
        national language
       </a>
       . In 1940, the
       <i>
        Balarílà ng Wikang Pambansâ
       </i>
       (English:
       <span lang="en" xml:lang="en">
        Grammar of the National Language
       </span>
       ) of grammarian
       <a href="/wiki/Lope_K._Santos" title="Lope K. Santos">
        Lope K. Santos
       </a>
       introduced the
       <b>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abakada_script" title="Abakada script">
         Abakada
        </a>
       </b>
       alphabet. This alphabet consists of 20 letters and became the standard alphabet of the national langauge.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebolusyon_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ebolusyon-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The orthography as used by Tagalog would eventually influence and spread to the systems of writing used by other Philippine languages (which had been using variants of the Spanish-based system of writing). In 1987, the ABAKADA was dropped and in its place is the expanded Filipino alphabet.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Baybayin">
        Baybayin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Baybayin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Baybayin" title="Baybayin">
        Baybayin
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Tagalog was written in an
       <a href="/wiki/Abugida" title="Abugida">
        abugida
       </a>
       —or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alphasyllabary" title="Alphasyllabary">
        alphasyllabary
       </a>
       —called
       <a href="/wiki/Baybayin" title="Baybayin">
        Baybayin
       </a>
       prior to the Spanish colonial period in the Philippines, in the 16th century. This particular
       <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">
        writing system
       </a>
       was composed of symbols representing three
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">
        vowels
       </a>
       and 14
       <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">
        consonants
       </a>
       . Belonging to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmic_family" title="Brahmic family">
        Brahmic family
       </a>
       of scripts, it shares similarities with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Kawi" title="Old Kawi">
        Old Kawi
       </a>
       script of
       <a href="/wiki/Javanese_language" title="Javanese language">
        Java
       </a>
       and is believed to be descended from the script used by the
       <a href="/wiki/Bugis" title="Bugis">
        Bugis
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Sulawesi" title="Sulawesi">
        Sulawesi
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Although it enjoyed a relatively high level of literacy, Baybayin gradually fell into disuse in favor of the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">
        Latin alphabet
       </a>
       taught by the Spaniards during their rule.
      </p>
      <p>
       There has been confusion of how to use Baybayin, which is actually an
       <a href="/wiki/Abugida" title="Abugida">
        abugida
       </a>
       , or an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alphasyllabary" title="Alphasyllabary">
        alphasyllabary
       </a>
       , rather than an
       <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">
        alphabet
       </a>
       . Not every letter in the Latin alphabet is represented with one of those in the Baybayin alphasyllabary. Rather than letters being put together to make sounds as in Western languages, Baybayin uses symbols to represent syllables.
      </p>
      <p>
       A "kudlit" resembling an apostrophe is used above or below a symbol to change the vowel sound after its consonant. If the kudlit is used above, the vowel is an "E" or "I" sound. If the kudlit is used below, the vowel is an "O" or "U" sound. A special kudlit was later added by Spanish missionaries in which a cross placed below the symbol to get rid of the vowel sound all together, leaving a consonant. Previously, the consonant without a following vowel was simply left out (for example,
       <i>
        bundok
       </i>
       being rendered as
       <i>
        budo
       </i>
       ), forcing the reader to use context when reading such words.
      </p>
      <p>
       Example:
      </p>
      <div style="text-align:center; margin:1em 0;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baybayin_sample_02.jpg" title="Ba Be Bo B (in Baybayin)">
        <img alt="Ba Be Bo B (in Baybayin)" data-file-height="65" data-file-width="191" height="65" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Baybayin_sample_02.jpg" width="191"/>
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Baybayin is
       <a class="external text" href="http://unicode.org/charts/PDF/U1700.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        encoded in Unicode
       </a>
       version 3.2 in the range 1700-171F under the name "Tagalog".
      </p>
      <div style="text-align:center; margin:1em 0;">

        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_alphabet">
        Latin alphabet
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Latin alphabet">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Abecedario">
        Abecedario
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Abecedario">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Until the first half of the 20th century, Tagalog was widely written in a variety of ways based on Spanish orthography consisting of 32 letters called '
       <b>
        ABECEDARIO'
       </b>
       (
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/abecedario#English-alphabet" title="wikt:abecedario">
        Spanish for "alphabet"
       </a>
       ):
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">

      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Abakada">
        Abakada
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Abakada">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Abakada_alphabet" title="Abakada alphabet">
        Abakada alphabet
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       When the national language was based on Tagalog, grammarian Lope K. Santos introduced a new alphabet consisting of 20 letters called
       <i>
        ABAKADA
       </i>
       in school grammar books called
       <i>
        balarilà
       </i>
       :
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">

      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Revised_alphabet">
        Revised alphabet
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Revised alphabet">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_alphabet" title="Filipino alphabet">
        Filipino alphabet
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1987, the Department of Education, Culture and Sports issued a memo stating that the Philippine alphabet had changed from the Pilipino-Tagalog Abakada version to a new 28-letter alphabet
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to make room for loans, especially family names from Spanish and English:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">

      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="ng_and_mga">
        <i>
         ng
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         mga
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: ng and mga">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ng_(digraph)" title="Ng (digraph)">
        ng (digraph)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">
        genitive
       </a>
       marker
       <i>
        ng
       </i>
       and the plural marker
       <i>
        mga
       </i>
       are abbreviations that are pronounced
       <i>
        nang
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [naŋ]
       </span>
       and
       <i>
        mangá
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [mɐˈŋa]
       </span>
       .
       <i>
        Ng
       </i>
       , in most cases, roughly translates to "of" (ex.
       <i>
        Siya ay kapatid
        <b>
         ng
        </b>
        nanay ko.
       </i>
       She is the sibling
       <i>
        of
       </i>
       my mother) while
       <i>
        nang
       </i>
       usually means "when" or can describe how something is done or to what extent (equivalent to the suffix
       <i>
        -ly
       </i>
       in English adverbs), among other uses.
       <i>
        Mga
       </i>
       (pronounced as "muh-NGA") denotes plurality as adding an
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        es
       </i>
       , or
       <i>
        ies
       </i>
       does in English (ex.
       <i>
        Iyan ang
        <b>
         mga
        </b>
        damit ko.
       </i>
       (Those are my clothe
       <b>
        s
       </b>
       )).
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         <b>
          Nang
         </b>
         si Hudas ay nadulás.
        </i>
        —When
        <a href="/wiki/Judas_Iscariot" title="Judas Iscariot">
         Judas
        </a>
        slipped.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Gumising siya
         <b>
          nang
         </b>
         <u>
          maaga
         </u>
         .
        </i>
        —He woke up
        <u>
         early
        </u>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Gumalíng
         <b>
          nang
         </b>
         <u>
          todo
         </u>
         si Juan dahil nag-ensayo siya.
        </i>
        —Juan
        <u>
         greatly
        </u>
        improved because he practiced.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In the first example,
       <i>
        nang
       </i>
       is used in lieu of the word
       <i>
        noong
       </i>
       (when;
       <i>
        <u>
         Noong
        </u>
        si Hudas ay madulas
       </i>
       ). In the second,
       <i>
        nang
       </i>
       describes that the person woke up (
       <i>
        gumising
       </i>
       ) early (
       <i>
        maaga
       </i>
       );
       <i>
        gumising nang maaga
       </i>
       . In the third,
       <i>
        nang
       </i>
       described up to what extent that Juan improved (
       <i>
        gumaling
       </i>
       ), which is "greatly" (
       <i>
        nang todo
       </i>
       ). In the latter two examples, the ligature
       <i>
        na
       </i>
       and its variants
       <i>
        -ng
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        -g
       </i>
       may also be used (
       <i>
        Gumising
        <u>
         na
        </u>
        maaga/Maaga
        <u>
         ng
        </u>
        gumising
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        Gumaling
        <u>
         na
        </u>
        todo/Todo
        <u>
         ng
        </u>
        gumaling
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The longer
       <i>
        nang
       </i>
       may also have other uses, such as a
       <a href="/wiki/Typographic_ligature" title="Typographic ligature">
        ligature
       </a>
       that joins a repeated word:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         Naghintáy sila
         <b>
          nang
         </b>
         naghintáy.
        </i>
        —They kept on waiting" (a closer calque: "They were waiting and waiting.")
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span id="p.C3.B4.2Fh.C3.B4_and_op.C3.B2.2Foh.C3.B2">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="pô/hô_and_opò/ohò">
        <i>
         pô/hô
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         opò/ohò
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: pô/hô and opò/ohò">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The words
       <i>
        pô/hô
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        opò/ohò
       </i>
       are traditionally used as polite iterations of the affirmative "
       <i>
        oo
       </i>
       " ("yes"). It is generally used when addressing elders or superiors such as bosses or teachers.
      </p>
      <p>
       "Pô" and "opò" are specifically used to denote a high level of respect when addressing older persons of close affinity like parents, relatives, teachers and family friends. "Hô" and "ohò" are generally used to politely address older neighbours, strangers, public officials, bosses and nannies, and may suggest a distance in societal relationship and respect determined by the addressee's social rank and not their age. However, "pô" and "opò" can be used in any case in order to express an elevation of respect.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Example: "
        <i>
         Pakitapon naman
         <b>
          pô/ho
         </b>
         yung basura.
        </i>
        " ("Please throw away the trash.")
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Used in the affirmative:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Ex: "
        <i>
         Gutóm ka na ba?" "
         <b>
          Opò/Ohò
         </b>
        </i>
        ". ("Are you hungry yet?" "Yes.")
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <i>
        Pô/Hô
       </i>
       may also be used in negation.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Ex: "
        <i>
         Hindi ko
         <b>
          pô/hô
         </b>
         alam 'yan.
        </i>
        " ("I don't know that.")
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary_and_borrowed_words">
        Vocabulary and borrowed words
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Vocabulary and borrowed words">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Indosphere" title="Indosphere">
        Indosphere
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Indianisation_(disambiguation)" title="Indianisation (disambiguation)">
        Indianisation
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_India-related_topics_in_the_Philippines" title="List of India-related topics in the Philippines">
        List of India-related topics in the Philippines
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Tagalog vocabulary is composed mostly of words of native Austronesian origin - most of the words that end with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diphthongs" title="Diphthongs">
        diphthongs
       </a>
       -iw, (e.g. saliw) and those words that exhibit
       <a href="/wiki/Reduplication" title="Reduplication">
        reduplication
       </a>
       (e.g. halo-halo, patpat, etc.). However it has a significant number of Spanish loanwords. Spanish is the language that has bequeathed the most loanwords to Tagalog. According to linguists, Spanish has even surpassed Malay in terms of loanwords borrowed. About 40% of everyday, informal Tagalog conversation is made up of Spanish loanwords.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Tagalog also includes many loanwords from English,
       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
        Indian
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">
        Vedic Sanskrit
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">
        Sanskrit
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">
        Tamil
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">
        Hokkien
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">
        Yue Chinese
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">
        Cantonese
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">
        Mandarin
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">
        Japanese
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
        Arabic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">
        Persian
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Due to trade with Mexico via the
       <a href="/wiki/Manila_galleon" title="Manila galleon">
        Manila galleons
       </a>
       from the 16th to the 19th centuries, many words from
       <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">
        Nahuatl
       </a>
       were introduced to Tagalog, but some of them were replaced by Spanish loanwords in the latter part of the Spanish colonization in the islands.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Philippines has long been a melting pot of nations. The islands have been subject to different influences and a meeting point of numerous migrations since the early prehistoric origins of trading activities, especially from the time of the Neolithic Period,
       <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">
        Silk Road
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">
        Tang Dynasty
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">
        Ming Dynasty
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Kingdom" title="Ryukyu Kingdom">
        Ryukyu Kingdom
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manila_Galleon" title="Manila Galleon">
        Manila Galleon
       </a>
       trading periods. This means that the evolution of the language is difficult to reconstruct (although many theories exist).
      </p>
      <p>
       In pre-Hispanic times,
       <a href="/wiki/Malay_trade_and_creole_languages" title="Malay trade and creole languages">
        Trade Malay
       </a>
       was widely known and spoken throughout
       <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">
        Maritime Southeast Asia
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       English has borrowed some words from Tagalog, such as abaca, barong, balisong, boondocks,
       <a href="/wiki/Jeepney" title="Jeepney">
        jeepney
       </a>
       , Manila hemp, pancit, ylang-ylang, and yaya, although the vast majority of these borrowed words are only used in the Philippines as part of the vocabularies of
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_English" title="Philippine English">
        Philippine English
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        Other examples of Tagalog words used in English
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Example
        </th>
        <th>
         Definition
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Boondocks" title="Boondocks">
          boondocks
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         meaning "rural" or "back country," was imported by American soldiers stationed in the Philippines following the Spanish–American War as a mispronounced version of the Tagalog
         <i>
          bundok
         </i>
         , which means "mountain."
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         cogon
        </td>
        <td>
         a type of grass, used for thatching. This word came from the Tagalog word
         <i>
          kugon
         </i>
         (a species of tall grass).
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ylang-ylang" title="Ylang-ylang">
          ylang-ylang
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         a tree whose fragrant flowers are used in perfumes.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abaca" title="Abaca">
          Abaca
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         a type of hemp fiber made from a plant in the banana family, from
         <i>
          abaká
         </i>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Manila_hemp" title="Manila hemp">
          Manila hemp
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         a light brown cardboard material used for folders and paper usually made from abaca hemp.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Windowpane_oyster" title="Windowpane oyster">
          Capiz
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         also known as window oyster, is used to make windows.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Tagalog has contributed several words to
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines" title="Spanish language in the Philippines">
        Philippine Spanish
       </a>
       , like
       <i>
        barangay
       </i>
       (from
       <i>
        balan͠gay,
       </i>
       meaning
       <i>
        barrio
       </i>
       ), the
       <i>
        abacá
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        cogon
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        palay
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        dalaga
       </i>
       etc.
      </p>
      <h3>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_literature">
        Religious literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Religious literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Religious literature remains one of the most dynamic contributors to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tagalog_literature" title="Tagalog literature">
        Tagalog literature
       </a>
       . The first Bible in Tagalog, then called
       <i>
        Ang Biblia
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ("the Bible") and now called
       <i>
        Ang Dating Biblia
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ("the Old Bible"), was published in 1905. In 1970, the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Philippine_Bible_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Philippine Bible Society (page does not exist)">
        Philippine Bible Society
       </a>
       translated the
       <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">
        Bible
       </a>
       into modern Tagalog. Even before the
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">
        Second Vatican Council
       </a>
       , devotional materials in Tagalog had been in circulation. There are at least four circulating Tagalog translations of the Bible
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        the
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Magandang_Balita_Biblia" title="Magandang Balita Biblia">
          Magandang Balita Biblia
         </a>
        </i>
        (a parallel translation of the
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Good_News_Bible" title="Good News Bible">
          Good News Bible
         </a>
        </i>
        ), which is the
        <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">
         ecumenical
        </a>
        version
       </li>
       <li>
        the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bibliya_ng_Sambayanang_Pilipino" title="Bibliya ng Sambayanang Pilipino">
         Bibliya ng Sambayanang Pilipino
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        the 1905
        <i>
         Ang Biblia
        </i>
        is a more Protestant version
       </li>
       <li>
        the
        <i>
         Bagong Sanlibutang Salin ng Banal na Kasulatan
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/New_World_Translation_of_the_Holy_Scriptures" title="New World Translation of the Holy Scriptures">
          New World Translation of the Holy Scriptures
         </a>
        </i>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       When the
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">
        Second Vatican Council
       </a>
       , (specifically the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacrosanctum_Concilium" title="Sacrosanctum Concilium">
        Sacrosanctum Concilium
       </a>
       ) permitted the universal prayers to be translated into
       <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">
        vernacular languages
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Bishops%27_Conference_of_the_Philippines" title="Catholic Bishops' Conference of the Philippines">
        Catholic Bishops' Conference of the Philippines
       </a>
       was one of the first to translate the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Missal" title="Roman Missal">
        Roman Missal
       </a>
       into Tagalog. The
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Missal" title="Roman Missal">
        Roman Missal
       </a>
       in Tagalog was published as early as 1982.
      </p>
      <p>
       Jehovah's Witnesses were printing Tagalog literature at least as early as 1941
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Watchtower" title="The Watchtower">
         The Watchtower
        </a>
       </i>
       (the primary magazine of Jehovah's Witnesses) has been published in Tagalog since at least the 1950s. New releases are now regularly released simultaneously in a number of languages, including Tagalog. The official website of Jehovah's Witnesses also has some publications available online in Tagalog.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Tagalog is quite a stable language, and very few revisions have been made to Catholic
       <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations" title="Bible translations">
        Bible translations
       </a>
       . Also, as Protestantism in the Philippines is relatively young,
       <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">
        liturgical
       </a>
       prayers tend to be more
       <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">
        ecumenical
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">
        Examples
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Examples">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span id="Lord.27s_Prayer">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lord's_Prayer">
        Lord's Prayer
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Lord's Prayer">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In Tagalog, the
       <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord's Prayer">
        Lord's Prayer
       </a>
       is exclusively known by its
       <a href="/wiki/Incipit" title="Incipit">
        incipit
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Amá Namin
       </i>
       (literally, "Our Father").
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        <i>
         Amá namin, sumasalangit Ka
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         Sambahín ang ngalan Mo.
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         Mapasaamin ang kaharián Mo.
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         Sundín ang loób Mo,
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         Dito sa lupà, gaya nang sa langit.
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         Bigyán Mo kamí ngayón ng aming kakanin sa araw-araw,
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         At patawarin Mo kamí sa aming mga salâ,
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         Para nang pagpápatawad namin,
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         Sa nagkakasalà sa amin;
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         At huwág Mo kamíng ipahintulot sa tuksó,
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         At iadyâ Mo kamí sa lahát ng masamâ.
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         [Sapagkát sa Inyó ang kaharián, at ang kapangyarihan,
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         At ang kaluwálhatian, ngayón, at magpakailanman.]
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         Amen
        </i>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights">
        Universal Declaration of Human Rights
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Universal Declaration of Human Rights">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       This is Article 1 of the
       <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">
        Universal Declaration of Human Rights
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Pángkalahatáng Pagpapahayag ng Karapatáng Pantao
       </i>
       )
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        <i>
         Bawat tao'y isinilang na may layà at magkakapantáy ang tagláy na dangál at karapatán. Silá'y pinagkalooban ng pangangatwiran at budhî na kailangang gamitin nilá sa pagtuturingan nilá sa diwà ng pagkakapatiran.
        </i>
        All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights. They are endowed with reason and conscience and should act towards one another in a spirit of brotherhood.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
         <a href="#cite_note-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers">
        Numbers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Numbers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The numbers (
       <i>
        Ang mga numero
       </i>
       ) in Tagalog language are of two sets. The first set consists of native Tagalog words and the other set are Spanish loanwords. (This may be compared to other East Asian languages, except with the second set of numbers borrowed from Spanish instead of Chinese.) For example, when a person refers to the number "seven", it can be translated into Tagalog as "
       <i>
        pito
       </i>
       " or "
       <i>
        syete
       </i>
       " (Spanish:
       <i>
        siete
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Number
        </th>

        </th>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Months_and_days">
        Months and days
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Months and days">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Months and days in Tagalog are also localised forms of Spanish months and days. "Month" in Tagalog is
       <i>
        buwán
       </i>
       (also the word for
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        moon
       </a>
       ) and "day" is
       <i>
        araw
       </i>
       (the word also means
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        sun
       </a>
       ). Unlike Spanish, however, months and days in Tagalog are always capitalised.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Time">
        Time
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Time">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Time expressions in Tagalog are also Tagalized forms of the corresponding Spanish. "Time" in Tagalog is
       <i>
        panahon
       </i>
       , or more commonly
       <i>
        oras
       </i>
       . Unlike Spanish and English, times in Tagalog are capitalized whenever they appear in a sentence.
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Common_phrases">
        Common phrases
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Common phrases">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="wikitable sortable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
        <th>
         Tagalog (with Pronunciation)
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Filipino
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Pilipino
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ˌpiːliˈpiːno]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         English
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Inglés
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ʔɪŋˈɡlɛs]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Tagalog
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Tagalog
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [tɐˈɡaːloɡ]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Spanish
        </td>
        <td>
         "Espanyol/Español"
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         What is your name?
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Anó ang pangalan ninyo/nila*?
         </i>
         (plural or polite)
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ɐˈno aŋ pɐˈŋaːlan nɪnˈjo]
         </span>
         ,
         <i>
          Anó ang pangalan mo?
         </i>
         (singular)
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ɐˈno aŋ pɐˈŋaːlan mo]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         How are you?
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          kumustá
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [kʊmʊsˈta]
         </span>
         (modern),
         <i>
          Anó po áng lagáy ninyo/nila?
         </i>
         (old use)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Good morning!
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Magandáng umaga!
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [mɐɡɐnˈdaŋ uˈmaːɡa]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Good noontime! (from 11 a.m. to 1 p.m.)
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Magandáng tanghali!
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [mɐɡɐnˈdaŋ taŋˈhaːlɛ]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Good afternoon! (from 1 p.m. to 6:00 p.m.)
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Magandáng hapon!
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [mɐɡɐnˈdaŋ ˈhaːpon]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Good evening!
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Magandáng gabí!
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [mɐɡɐnˈdaŋ ɡɐˈbɛ]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Good-bye
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          paálam
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [pɐˈʔaːlam]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Please
        </td>
        <td>
         Depending on the nature of the verb, either
         <i>
          pakí-
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [pɐˈki]
         </span>
         or
         <i>
          makí-
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [mɐˈki]
         </span>
         is attached as a prefix to a verb.
         <i>
          ngâ
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ŋaʔ]
         </span>
         is optionally added after the verb to increase politeness. (e.g.
         <i>
          Pakipasa ngâ ang tinapay.
         </i>
         ("Can you pass the bread, please?"))
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Thank you
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Salamat
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [sɐˈlaːmat]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         This one
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ito
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ʔiˈtoh]
         </span>
         , sometimes pronounced
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ʔɛˈtoh]
         </span>
         (literally—"it", "this")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         That one
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          iyan
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ʔiˈjan]
         </span>
         , When pointing to something at greater distances:
         <i>
          iyun
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ʔiˈjʊn]
         </span>
         or
         <i>
          iyon
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ʔiˈjon]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Here
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          dito
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [dɪˈtoh]
         </span>
         ,
         <i>
          heto
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [hɛˈtoh]
         </span>
         ("Here it is")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         There
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          doon
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [dʒan]
         </span>
         ,
         <i>
          hayan
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [hɑˈjan]
         </span>
         ("There it is")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         How much?
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Magkano?
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [mɐɡˈkaːno]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Yes
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          oo
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ˈoːʔo]
         </span>
         <p>
          <i>
           opô
          </i>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [ˈʔopoʔ]
          </span>
          or
          <i>
           ohô
          </i>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [ˈʔohoʔ]
          </span>
          (formal/polite form)
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         No
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          hindî
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [hɪnˈdɛʔ]
         </span>
         , often shortened to
         <i>
          dî
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [dɛʔ]
         </span>
         <p>
          <i>
           hindî pô
          </i>
          (formal/polite form)
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         I don't know
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          hindî ko álam
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [hɪnˈdɛʔ ko aːlam]
         </span>
         <p>
          Very informal:
          <i>
           ewan
          </i>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [ʔɛˈʊɑn]
          </span>
          , archaic
          <i>
           aywan
          </i>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [ɑjˈʊɑn]
          </span>
          (closest English equivalent: colloquial dismissive 'Whatever')
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Sorry
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          pasensya pô
         </i>
         (literally from the word "patience") or
         <i>
          paumanhin po
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [pɐˈsɛːnʃa poʔ]
         </span>
         <i>
          patawad po
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [pɐtaːwad poʔ]
         </span>
         (literally—"asking your forgiveness")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Because
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          kasí
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [kɐˈsɛ]
         </span>
         or
         <i>
          dahil
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [dɑˈhɪl]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Hurry!
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          dalí!
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [dɐˈli]
         </span>
         ,
         <i>
          bilís!
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [bɪˈlis]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Again
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          mulí
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [muˈli]
         </span>
         ,
         <i>
          ulít
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ʊˈlɛt]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         I don't understand
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Hindî ko naiintindihan
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [hɪnˈdiː ko nɐʔɪɪnˌtɪndiˈhan]
         </span>
         or
         <p>
          <i>
           Hindi ko nauunawaan
          </i>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [hɪnˈdiː ko nɐʔʊʊnawaʔˌʔan]
          </span>
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         What?
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Anó?
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ɐˈno]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Where?
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Saán?
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [sɐˈʔan]
         </span>
         ,
         <i>
          Nasaán?
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ˌnaːsɐˈʔan]
         </span>
         (literally - "Where at?")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Why?
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Bakít?
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [bɑˈkɛt]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         When?
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Kailan?
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [kɑjˈlɑn]
         </span>
         ,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [kɑˈɪˈlɑn]
         </span>
         , or
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [kɛˈlɑn]
         </span>
         (literally—"In what order?/"At what count?"")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         How?
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Paánó?
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [pɑˌɐˈno]
         </span>
         (literally—"By what?")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Where's the bathroom?
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Nasaán ang banyo?
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ˌnaːsɐˈʔan ʔaŋ ˈbaːnjo]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          Generic toast
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mabuhay_(expression)" title="Mabuhay (expression)">
           Mabuhay
          </a>
          !
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [mɐˈbuːhaɪ]
         </span>
         (literally—"long live")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Do you speak English?
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Marunong ka bang magsalitâ ng Ingglés?
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [mɐˈɾuːnoŋ ka baŋ mɐɡsaliˈtaː naŋ ʔɪŋˈɡlɛs]
         </span>
         <p>
          <i>
           Marunong po ba kayong magsalitâ ng Ingglés?
          </i>
          (polite version for elders and strangers)
          <br/>
          <i>
           Marunong ka bang mag-Ingglés?
          </i>
          (short form)
          <br/>
          <i>
           Marunong po ba kayong mag-Ingglés?
          </i>
          (short form, polite version for elders and strangers)
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         It is fun to live.
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Masaya ang mabuhay!
         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [mɐˈsaˈja ʔaŋ mɐˈbuːhaɪ]
         </span>
         or
         <i>
          Masaya'ng mabuhay
         </i>
         (contracted version)
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <small>
        *Pronouns such as
        <i>
         niyo
        </i>
        (2nd person plural) and
        <i>
         nila
        </i>
        (3rd person plural) are used on a single 2nd person in polite or formal language. See
        <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_grammar" title="Tagalog grammar">
         Tagalog grammar
        </a>
        .
       </small>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Proverbs">
        Proverbs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tagalog_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Proverbs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <i>
        Ang hindî marunong lumingón sa pinánggalingan ay hindî makaráratíng sa paroroonan
       </i>
       . (José Rizal)
       <br/>
       One who knows not how to look back from whence he came, will never get to where he is going.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Tao ka nang humarap, bilang tao kitang haharapin.
       </i>
       <br/>
       (A proverb in Southern Tagalog that made people aware the significance of sincerity in Tagalog communities. It says, "As a human you reach me, I treat you as a human and never act as a traitor.")
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Hulí man daw at magalíng, nakákahábol pa rin. (Hulí man raw at magalíng, nakákahabol pa rin.)
       </i>
       <br/>
       If one is behind but capable, one will still be able to catch up.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Magbirô ka na sa lasíng, huwág lang sa bagong gising
       </i>
       .
       <br/>
       Make fun of someone drunk, if you must, but never one who has just awakened.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Aanhín pa ang damó kung patáy na ang kabayo?
       </i>
       <br/>
       What use is the grass if the horse is already dead?
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Ang sakít ng kalingkingan, ramdám ng buóng katawán.
       </i>
       <br/>
       The pain in the pinkie is felt by the whole body.
       <br/>
       (In a group, if one goes down, the rest follow.)
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Nasa hulí ang pagsisisi.
       </i>
       <br/>
       Regret is always in the end.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Pagkáhabà-habà man ng prusisyón, sa simbahan pa rin ang tulóy.
       </i>
       <br/>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Procession" title="Procession">
        procession
       </a>
       may stretch on and on, but it still ends up at the church.
       <br/>
       (In romance: refers to how certain people are destined to be married. In general: refers to how some things are inevitable, no matter how long you try and postpone it.)
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Kung 'dî mádaán sa santóng dasalan, daanin sa santóng paspasan.
       </i>
       <br/>
       If it cannot be got through holy prayer, get it through blessed force.
       <br/>
       (In romance and courting:
       <i>
        santóng paspasan
       </i>
       literally means 'holy speeding' and is a euphemism for
       <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">
        sexual intercourse
       </a>
       . It refers to the two styles of courting by Filipino boys: one is the traditional, protracted, restrained manner favoured by older generations, which often featured
       <a href="/wiki/Harana_(serenade)" title="Harana (serenade)">
        serenades
       </a>
       and manual labour for the girl's parents; the other is upfront seduction, which may lead to a slap on the face or a pregnancy out of wedlock. The second conclusion is known as
       <i>
        pikot
       </i>
       or what Western cultures would call a '
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shotgun_marriage" title="Shotgun marriage">
        shotgun marriage
       </a>
       '. This proverb is also applied in terms of diplomacy and negotiation.)
      </p>
